After making a comparison of the genetic spacing based on Y chromosomal haplogroups (FST), it was determined that Assyrian population in Armenia are closely related to the Armenians of <a href="/wiki/Syunik_Province" title="Syunik Province">Syunik</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karabakh" title="Karabakh">Karabakh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Armenia" title="Eastern Armenia">eastern Armenia</a>, and are genetically distant from the four Arabic-speaking peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Arab" class="mw-redirect" title="Palestinian Arab">Palestinian Arabs</a>, Syrians and <a href="/wiki/Yemenis" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemenis">Yemenis</a>, who collectively display a quite distinguished cluster of comparative data sets. Study by Lashgary et al. (2011) showcased certain connection of Assyrians to Armenians and some other <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups in Iran">peoples from Iran</a> according to Y-chromosomal single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Haplogroups">Haplogroups</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Y-DNA_haplogroup" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-DNA haplogroup">Y-DNA haplogroup</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_T-M184" title="Haplogroup T-M184">T-M184</a> (reported as K*) has been measured at 15.09% among <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Armenia" title="Assyrians in Armenia">Assyrians in Armenia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>  The haplogroup is frequent in <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Eastern Jews">Middle Eastern Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Georgian people">Georgians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Druze people">Druze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Somali people">Somalians</a>. According to a 2011 study by Lashgary et al.,  <a href="/wiki/R1b" class="mw-redirect" title="R1b">R1b</a> has been measured at 40% among <a href="/wiki/Assyrians_in_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="Assyrians in Iran">Assyrians in Iran</a>, making it major haplogroup among Iranian Assyrians.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> Yet another <a href="/wiki/DNA_test" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA test">DNA test</a> comprising 48 Assyrian male subjects from Iran, the Y-DNA haplogroups <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J_(Y-DNA)" title="Haplogroup J (Y-DNA)">J-M304</a>, found in its greatest concentration in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, and the northern <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R-M269" title="Haplogroup R-M269">R-M269</a>, were also frequent at 29.2% each.<sup id="cite_ref-Grugni_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grugni-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Lashgary et al. explain the presence of haplogroup R in Iranian Assyrians and Assyrians in general as a consequence of mixing with <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, assimilation/integration of different people carrying haplogroup R, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a> due to small population size and endogamy due to religious barriers.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Haplogroup <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J-M172" title="Haplogroup J-M172">J2</a> has been measured at 13.4%, which is commonly found in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, coastal <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_plateau" class="mw-redirect" title="Iranian plateau">Iranian plateau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.scribd.com/document/149115268/National-Identity-in-Neo-Assyrian-Empire">"National and Ethnic Identity in the Neo-Assyrian Empire and Assyrian Identity in Post-Empire Times"</a>. Simo Parpola. p.&#160;20. <q>When the Seleucid Empire disintegrated at the end of the second century BC, its western remnants were annexed to Rome, while several semi-independent kingdoms of decidedly Assyrian stamp and/or identity (Osrhoene, Adiabene, Hatra, Assur) popped up in the East under Parthian overlordship. These kingdoms perpetuated Assyrian cultural and religious traditions but were also receptive to Christianity, whose central ideas were in line with the central tenets of Assyrian religion and ideology, and which was felt as intrinsically Assyrian because of the Aramaic affinity of Jesus and the disciples.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=National+and+Ethnic+Identity+in+the+Neo-Assyrian+Empire+and+Assyrian+Identity+in+Post-Empire+Times&amp;rft.pages=20&amp;rft.pub=Simo+Parpola&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.scribd.com%2Fdocument%2F149115268%2FNational-Identity-in-Neo-Assyrian-Empire&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyrian+people" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Iran_and_the_Caucasus-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Iran_and_the_Caucasus_217-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.rau.am/downloads/publ.kafedr/episkoposyan_medbiolog/Yepiskoposian_I&amp;C_06.pdf">Yepiskoposian et al., Iran and the Caucasus, Volume 10, Number 2, 2006, pp. 191-208(18), "Genetic Testing of Language Replacement Hypothesis in Southwest Asia"</a></span>

<li id="cite_note-pubmed.gov-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-pubmed.gov_218-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Banoei, M. M.; Chaleshtori, M. H.; Sanati, M. H.; Shariati, P; Houshmand, M; Majidizadeh, T; Soltani, N. J.; Golalipour, M (Feb 2008). "Variation of DAT1 VNTR alleles and genotypes among old ethnic groups in Mesopotamia to the Oxus region". <i>Hum Biol</i>. <b>80</b> (1): 73–81. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.3378%2F1534-6617%282008%2980%5B73%3AVODVAA%5D2.0.CO%3B2">10.3378/1534-6617(2008)80&#91;73:VODVAA&#93;2.0.CO;2</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" class="mw-redirect" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/18505046">18505046</a>. <q>The relationship probability was lowest between Assyrians and other communities. <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">Endogamy</a> was found to be high for this population through determination of the heterogeneity coefficient (+0,6867), Our study supports earlier findings indicating the relatively closed nature of the Assyrian community as a whole, which as a result of their religious and cultural traditions, have had little intermixture with other populations.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Hum+Biol&amp;rft.atitle=Variation+of+DAT1+VNTR+alleles+and+genotypes+among+old+ethnic+groups+in+Mesopotamia+to+the+Oxus+region&amp;rft.volume=80&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=73-81&amp;rft.date=2008-02&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3378%2F1534-6617%282008%2980%5B73%3AVODVAA%5D2.0.CO%3B2&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F18505046&amp;rft.aulast=Banoei&amp;rft.aufirst=M.+M.&amp;rft.au=Chaleshtori%2C+M.+H.&amp;rft.au=Sanati%2C+M.+H.&amp;rft.au=Shariati%2C+P&amp;rft.au=Houshmand%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Majidizadeh%2C+T&amp;rft.au=Soltani%2C+N.+J.&amp;rft.au=Golalipour%2C+M&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAssyrian+people" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-BMC_Evolutionary_Biology_219-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.biomedcentral.com/content/pdf/1471-2148-11-288.pdf">Al-Zahery et al., BMC Evolutionary Biology 2011, 11:288, "In search of the genetic footprints of Sumerians: a survey of Y-chromosome and mtDNA variation in the Marsh Arabs of Iraq"</a>"In the less frequent J1-M267* clade, only marginally affected by events of expansion, Marsh Arabs shared haplotypes with other Iraqi and Assyrian samples, supporting a common local background."</span>
</li>
